<div id="review_8147342" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8147342" />
    <a title="J.G. Keely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely"><img alt="J.G. Keely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8147342?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.G. Keely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.G. Keely" href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely">J.G. Keely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=contemporary-fantasy">contemporary-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8147342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1407416558823339848">It is unfortunate that Gaiman seems to be unable to surrender his archetypal cast to either humanity or inhumanity, but lets them sit awkwardly in the middle. Though he often presents Dream and his siblings as falling to love or petty squabbling, their reactions to such are often not to work towards decision, but to subside. In those cases where they do act, it becomes merely a meaningless exercise to continue the story. When this is done for the purpose of framing other tales and interweaving i</span>
  <span id="freeText1407416558823339848" style="display:none">It is unfortunate that Gaiman seems to be unable to surrender his archetypal cast to either humanity or inhumanity, but lets them sit awkwardly in the middle. Though he often presents Dream and his siblings as falling to love or petty squabbling, their reactions to such are often not to work towards decision, but to subside. In those cases where they do act, it becomes merely a meaningless exercise to continue the story. When this is done for the purpose of framing other tales and interweaving ideas, it does not bother so much, but when it is the story itself, it loses that edge.<br /><br />Dream, like most if not all of Gaiman's protagonists, seems to operate merely as an oculus for the reader, and we often find his own chance at decision revoked. The same is true of American Gods or Neverwhere, where any conflict set up against the main character tends to be resolved without growth or change since there is no decision made.<br /><br />It is perhaps Gaiman's reticence on these archetypal characters which provides that the dialogue of this collection is often ungainly and without art. Gaiman works better when knee-deep in humanity than when trying to work beyond it.<br /><br /><b>
  <a href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2013/09/suggested-readings-in-comics.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My Suggested Reading In Comics</a>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1407416558823339848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1644980780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1644980780" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1644980780?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18911763-season-of-mists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=horror-occult">horror-occult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dark-fantasy-gothic">dark-fantasy-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1644980780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5250283625273844996"><b>The Sandman Vol. 4: Season of Mists: Deities scramble to fill a vacancy in Hell </b><br/><br/>After the stand-alone stories of Vol 3, many of which only feature Morpheus in the background, in Vol 4 the Sandman takes center stage once again. The Prologue sets the stage for a new story-arc, as Destiny strolls through his barren garden, in his monk’s cowl and with his huge book, and encounters the three Fates. As usual, they drop some cryptic clues that big events are afoot and then depart. Destiny checks his bo</span>
  <span id="freeText5250283625273844996" style="display:none"><b>The Sandman Vol. 4: Season of Mists: Deities scramble to fill a vacancy in Hell </b><br /><br />After the stand-alone stories of Vol 3, many of which only feature Morpheus in the background, in Vol 4 the Sandman takes center stage once again. The Prologue sets the stage for a new story-arc, as Destiny strolls through his barren garden, in his monk’s cowl and with his huge book, and encounters the three Fates. As usual, they drop some cryptic clues that big events are afoot and then depart. Destiny checks his book, and learns that the Endless must hold a family meeting. And so the story begins…<br /><br />Neil Gaiman’s love of stories, mythology, deities, demons, and mysterious unseen forces is plain to see. In Season of Mists, he treats us to a very unique perspective on these things by lifting the curtain that mortals rarely see beyond, and switching the perspective to that of the deities and mythological creatures that have populated the minds of mankind since time immemorial. <br /><br />There is something fundamental in humanity that strives to personify such forces in anthropomorphic form, and Gaiman gleefully takes elements of some of well-known belief systems such as Christianity, which appears to have top billing (including oblique references to the unseen Creator), but includes cameos from an impressive A-list of deities such as Odin, Thor, Loki (Norse), Anubis, Bast, Bes (Egyptian), Susano-o-no-mikoto (Shinto), Azazel, Choronzon, and Merkin (demons from Hell), a Lord of Chaos and Lord of Order, the Faerie siblings Cluracan and Nuala, and two Angels from the Silver City named Duma and Remiel.<br /><br />You see, all these supernatural beings have come to call on Morpheus’ palace at the heart of The Dreaming because Lucifer, the fallen Angel, has decided after uncountable millennia that he has tired of overseeing the punishment of the damned, and has driven out from Hell both the damned (his clients) and the demons who torment them (his staff). Morpheus only discovers this after deciding he has wronged his former lover Nada, the African queen he damned to hell 10,000 years earlier. When Morpheus arrives in Hell to free her, Lucifer is just tidying up some stragglers and has a convivial chat with Morpheus (who was prepared for a fight), ending with him handing over the key to Hell and heading off to parts unknown (which is the start of a spinoff series called Lucifer).<br /><br />What a great story idea - Lucifer quitting his job and handing it off to Morpheus unexpectedly. So the bulk of the story involves all the various divinities entreating Dream why they most deserve to take over Hell, as Morpheus clearly isn’t keen on overseeing it. They offers bribes, threats, and stratagems and confidential negotiations abound. It’s all done marvelously and matter-of-fact. I’m not sure how a religious reader would react to this - would this story be offensive? Within the context of the story, Gaiman grants equal weight to each god’s pantheon, but clearly the Creator and his Angels have power and authority above the others. <br /><br />Which raises some very obvious questions - why does the Creator stay in the background? If we go by the number of believers in the world today, why are Jesus, Allah, and the Buddha not present? And how do The Endless fit into these pantheons? Who sets the hierarchical rules among these competing deities? Clearly there are some hard and fast rules that the Endless such as Destiny, Dream, and Death must follow. They have their duties that must be carried out. <br /><br />But who made these rules? Are all these deities simply manifestations of humanity’s imagination? Or fundamental concepts that predate humanity? Are they fundamental elements of the universe, or do they only exist on Earth because we believe in them? Would all these powerful beings disappear if humanity stopped believing in them, or if humanity wiped itself out? Gaiman’s SANDMAN series raises all kinds of fascinating philosophical questions while still delivering quirky, frightening, and melancholy stories. It makes me very curious how many of these questions he might answer in the course of the series. Can any such questions ever really be answered to everyone’s satisfaction?<br /><br />In the end, I think human religions are mostly inward-looking, giving theological ideas human form, scale and relevance. In my opinion, none of them adequately address the observable universe, let alone our Milky Way galaxy, filled with uncountable stars, black holes, dark matter, and empty spaces, things that make human spiritual affairs and the Earth itself look very tiny indeed. If I were to ever believe in a religion, it would have to take all those things into account. The only SF philosophical treatment of the universe I have found compelling remains Olaf Stapledon’s two masterworks from the 1930s, Last and First Men and Star Maker. But I digress. The SANDMAN is focused on the supernatural manifestations of humankind’s imagination, giving them real form in a higher realm, and it is an impressive and thoughtful series that I am enjoying a lot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5250283625273844996" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2996213720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14871185969828063603">My favorite since the first one.<br/><br/>It's been over thirty years since this was published, and it still managed to do things that surprised me. Morpheus, or Dream the Endless, has been informed that consigning his human lover Nada to hell for 10,000 years for the crime of rejecting him and wounding his pride was a not great thing to do, so he sets out to make things as right as he can, which means once again descending into hell and facing Lucifer. He makes his preparations, says his goodbyes, fully </span>
  <span id="freeText14871185969828063603" style="display:none">My favorite since the first one.<br><br>It's been over thirty years since this was published, and it still managed to do things that surprised me. Morpheus, or Dream the Endless, has been informed that consigning his human lover Nada to hell for 10,000 years for the crime of rejecting him and wounding his pride was a not great thing to do, so he sets out to make things as right as he can, which means once again descending into hell and facing Lucifer. He makes his preparations, says his goodbyes, fully expecting that he will have to fight Lucifer, and that he might lose. But when he gets there, things don't go as expected.<br><br>Spoilers ahoy.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1569877602i/28224357._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>When he gets to Hell, he finds it empty. Lucifer has quit, and released the denizens to go where they may, including Nada. When I say he quit, I mean he has decided to no longer reign over Hell, and he's just done. He wants to go lay on a beach somewhere and watch the sunset (he does). And he gives the key to Hell to Morpheus to do with as he wishes. This is the form his revenge takes. <br><br>First, I kept thinking Lucifer was playing a trick. Then I realized he wasn't, and then I could only think, <i>Wait, can he do this? He's doing this!</i> The rest of the story became about the fallout. What will Morpheus do with Hell? Who will he give its care over to? (There are many factions who show up at his realm begging an audience, wanting to bargain with him and plead their case as to why Hell should be given to them.) How can he find Nada now?<br><br>Several things he does here will most likely have lasting consequences for the story, but it works on its own as a complete arc as well. It also opens up some very interesting paths for Lucifer as a character (no doubt this is why he has is own comic, which I also haven't read, but now am more interested in than I was before).<br><br>Side note, the edition I read has a truly execrable introduction by Harlan Ellison, in which he only manages to praise Gaiman and his story by putting down literally everyone else, and making himself look like a first class wanker in the process. <br><br>[4.5 stars]</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_484243647" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243647" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484243647?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857682345" href="/book/show/9918498-season-of-mists">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484243647" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1805068054228176894">The fourth <i>Sandman</i> volume, collecting a seven-part story arc, revisits Queen Nada, the woman who rejected Morpheus ten thousand years ago and spent that time condemned to Hell for it. In a fantastic prologue, we are finally introduced to the remaining Endless siblings when the oldest, Destiny, calls for a family meeting in his realm—where it is pointed out to Morpheus that he may have overreacted. To right this wrong, Dream resolves to return to Hell to forgive and free Nada... but Lucifer hatch</span>
  <span id="freeText1805068054228176894" style="display:none">The fourth <i>Sandman</i> volume, collecting a seven-part story arc, revisits Queen Nada, the woman who rejected Morpheus ten thousand years ago and spent that time condemned to Hell for it. In a fantastic prologue, we are finally introduced to the remaining Endless siblings when the oldest, Destiny, calls for a family meeting in his realm—where it is pointed out to Morpheus that he may have overreacted. To right this wrong, Dream resolves to return to Hell to forgive and free Nada... but Lucifer hatches his own plan and abdicates, leaving Dream the key to an empty Hell.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Sometimes we can choose the paths we follow. Sometimes our choices are made for us. And sometimes we have no choice at all."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I absolutely adore the character of Lucifer Morningstar, and find episode 2 of this story arc to be the strongest—I can't have been the only one to feel this way, since he acquired his own DC spin-off series based on the events presented here. Apart from Lucifer's character, who is obviously based on Christianity, <i>Season of Mists</i> features players from other world mythologies and religions, such as Thor, Odin, and Loki from Norse mythology, Anubis, Bast, and Bes from Egyptian mythology, Susanoo-no-Mikoto from Japanese mythology, as well as representatives from the Faerie realm, and the DC personifications of the Lords of Chaos and Order, respectively, who all come to the Dreaming to bribe, threaten, or bargain for the suddenly empty piece of real estate Morpheus finds himself a reluctant keeper of. It's a trans-pantheistic crossover that works much better than one might think, an approach Gaiman is clearly fascinated by, since he comes back to it in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1932306832" rel="nofollow noopener">American Gods</a>
</i> years later.<br /><br />If there's a taint on this story arc, it's that it drags a little in the second half following an interlude (episode 4) which, while not <i>bad</i>, can only be described as a filler issue with some of the worst art to be found throughout the whole series—it looks unfinished at the best of times, and while it showcases some of the earthly consequences of an empty Hell, it's overall pretty weak.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"I think hell is something you carry around with you. Not somewhere you go."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />The biggest theme of <i>Season of Mists</i> is that of responsibility—we come across the so far second reference to an Endless sibling who abandoned his realm, and of course Lucifer's abandonment of his duties triggers big changes that must be balanced. This is where everything that's been set up in the <i>Sandman</i> canon so far starts weaving together—brilliantly so.<br /><br />—————<br /><br />My other ડꪖꪀᦔꪑꪖꪀ reviews:<br /><br />01: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243577" rel="nofollow noopener">Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</a> · ★★★½<br />02: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243593" rel="nofollow <span id="freeTextContainer5637545193495436520">Sandman continues pushing into new territory as we get a confrontation I wasn't expecting. Lucifer and Dream. <br/><br/>See it starts off with Dream and all his siblings getting together. He reflects on his pass action of sending Nada, a mortal he once loved to hell. In doing so he decides he must travel to hell to rescue her. A typical mission of a "hero" on his way to save his long lost "lover" for condemning her to hell turns into a very interesting "Who rules what now" in a way I never expected. <br/><br/>The f</span>
  <span id="freeText5637545193495436520" style="display:none">Sandman continues pushing into new territory as we get a confrontation I wasn't expecting. Lucifer and Dream. <br /><br />See it starts off with Dream and all his siblings getting together. He reflects on his pass action of sending Nada, a mortal he once loved to hell. In doing so he decides he must travel to hell to rescue her. A typical mission of a "hero" on his way to save his long lost "lover" for condemning her to hell turns into a very interesting "Who rules what now" in a way I never expected. <br /><br />The first half is great, but it's the second half that elevates this to new heights. When Dream has to decide who will take over hell. This creates a lot of piece of shit gods/monsters coming out of the woodwork to try and claim their right to the domain. This also leaves Dream to make some big choices and very interesting ones. <br /><br />My only negative is issue 4 I believe, which felt so out of left field. I don't even believe it was written by Gaiman. It was about a boy who I believe could see the dead and gets bullied to the point of something happening. It wasn't bad but smacked right between two major arcs did it no favors and my least interesting part. <br /><br />Still, this was amazing. A very easy 4.5 out of 5. </span>

<div id="review_105056171" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105056171" />
    <a title="Jesse Field" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3633038-jesse-field"><img alt="Jesse Field" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1273174024p2/3633038.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105056171?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesse Field" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesse Field" href="/user/show/3633038-jesse-field">Jesse Field</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105056171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15546760150585568062">"But even if Destiny could tell you, he will not. Destiny holds his secrets. The Garden of Destiny. You would know it if you saw it. After all, you wander it until you die. Or Beyond. For the paths are long, and even in death, there is no ending to them."<br/><br/>Ugh.<br/><br/>It's really too bad I didn't get a chance to read Sandman when I was about 14. Gaiman's clunky, endless scene-setting ("assorted events are set in motion; and a relationship thought long done with proves to have much relevance today") is mea</span>
  <span id="freeText15546760150585568062" style="display:none">"But even if Destiny could tell you, he will not. Destiny holds his secrets. The Garden of Destiny. You would know it if you saw it. After all, you wander it until you die. Or Beyond. For the paths are long, and even in death, there is no ending to them."<br /><br />Ugh.<br /><br />It's really too bad I didn't get a chance to read Sandman when I was about 14. Gaiman's clunky, endless scene-setting ("assorted events are set in motion; and a relationship thought long done with proves to have much relevance today") is meant to enthrall adolescents trying their best to be what was once called "alternative." Reading Gaiman is much like listening to Nirvana or Smashing Pumpkins, or watching early Tim Burton's 1988 <i>Batman</i> -- what once captivated now at first jogs our interest, but then quickly loses it again.<br /><br />Two quick notes, one positive and one negative, that I want to carry away down the path towards making my own creative writing:<br /><br />1. Gaiman's pacing is slow. He repeats himself (did you know that in Dream's kingdom, Dream can do anything he wants? Yep! ...Hey! Did you know...) He doesn't always use "exposition as ammunition," but instead inserts exposition in between when major action should be happening, deliberately slowing the pace further. There are no real climaxes in Gaiman's fiction: protagonists face big decisions, but then something else takes care of it all for them. Dream expects to have a crisis, but Lucifer preempts Dream by abandoning Hell. Dream expects to have a crisis again, but Creation sends in his angels to take the keys to Hell. Daemon ex Machina, Deus ex Machina: this volume is nothing more than a double ex Machina. The pace of the work is thus made loose and slack, like a middle school sagger shlumping to Shpongle.<br /><br />2. Graphic novels have a dual center: text, art. Here, the protagonist Dream is a shadowy and sullen artibus with a conscience. Text-wise, there isn't a lot to him. That thus leaves all the room necessary for a whole team to go in and render Dream in pencil and ink. If Gaiman has any real talent, it's in writing loose text that leaves room. He's a natural minimalist, suggesting whole worlds in breathy asides ("There is <b>much</b> that Faerie can offer you.") It would be nice if there was more than a suggestion at times, and here again, the art saves, with fantastic drawings of the castles of Hell, etc. This particular series combines multiple artistic idioms purposely to suggest an uneasy collection of worlds -- the gods of Nippon stand next to the Gods of Egypt, etc. As a study in composing portraits to form layered, interwoven settings, <i>Sandman</i> could serve as a representative example that rarely goes wrong.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15546760150585568062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_967064947" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/967064947" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/967064947?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781563890413" href="/book/show/104596.Season_of_Mists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=library-borrow">library-borrow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer967064947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14828607790776063393">As I had hoped after reading Volume Three, the fourth volume does get back into the meat of the story and we learn more about the Endless, which is what I've been waiting for all along. This quaint little family convinces Dream that this thing he did a bazillion years ago was totally uncool and he's sort of a douche for it, and he's all "Really? Huh, I <i>gueeeessss...</i>" and he goes off to make things all better again. But it's not just a matter of strolling into Hell and fixing shit, because, well,</span>
  <span id="freeText14828607790776063393" style="display:none">As I had hoped after reading Volume Three, the fourth volume does get back into the meat of the story and we learn more about the Endless, which is what I've been waiting for all along. This quaint little family convinces Dream that this thing he did a bazillion years ago was totally uncool and he's sort of a douche for it, and he's all "Really? Huh, I <i>gueeeessss...</i>" and he goes off to make things all better again. But it's not just a matter of strolling into Hell and fixing shit, because, well, it's <i>Hell</i>, so, y'know, there's some face-time with Lucifer, and then there's a small matter of the key to Hell and what to do with it, and then there's a random issue involving some boys who are ghosts.<br /><br />And what I really realized is that this is volume is almost incoherent. It doesn't mean it sucks or that I didn't like it, because it doesn't and I did. But it's really all over the place, the panels jump from character to character, with not a lot of explanation as to what is really happening. I feel the artwork is (for lack of a proper word) sloppier than it has been in the past, and that's disappointing. Why does Dream no longer look like Robert Smith so much? Who's got a problem with looking at a Robert Smith visage?<br /><br />The story is definitely taking off here, and there's a lot of information crammed into a small amount of space. It's all probably important so I'll take it in stride, but it's not my favorite. I'm giving it four stars merely because of the introduction to the Endless and the insinuation that there's another Endless-sibling out there that no one knows where exactly (except for Bast because of course), but suddenly Dream is all "Nah, I respect his privacy", Mr. Good-Guy about it all.<br /><br />But otherwise, had those introductions not taken place, I think this would have been a solid 3-star read for me. More of the Endless, yes. More of that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14828607790776063393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wz7kXRMf871OCvdJ4cGPgwoOB15zBuOHSuUPCw2jJ2/ErjRxW+WbEJhVQTzNSMtDuriXrMyqhrPeydkrtahXhw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

As civil society crumbles around him, Bruce Wayne\'s long-suppressed vigilante side finally breaks free of its self-imposed shackles. \n\nThe Dark Knight returns in a blaze of fury, taking on a whole new generation of criminals and matching their level of violence. He is soon joined by this generation\'s Robin—a girl named Carrie Kelley, who proves to be just as invaluable as her predecessors.\n\nBut can Batman and Robin deal with the threat posed by their deadliest enemies, after years of incarceration have made them into perfect psychopaths? And more important, can anyone survive the coming fallout of an undeclared war between the superpowers—or a clash of what were once the world\'s greatest superheroes?\n\nOver fifteen years after its Craig Russell, Shawn McManus, John Watkiss, Jill Thompson, Duncan Eagleso<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17622519705837306344\" style=\"display:none\">Fables &amp; Reflections (1993) is the sixth collection of issues in the DC Comics series, The Sandman. It was written by Neil Gaiman and illustrated by Bryan Talbot, Stan Woch, P. Craig Russell, Shawn McManus, John Watkiss, Jill Thompson, Duncan Eagleson, Kent Williams, Mark Buckingham, Vince Locke and Dick Giordano, coloured by Danny Vozzo and Lovern Kindzierski/Digital Chameleon, and lettered by Todd Klein. The introduction is written by Gene Wolfe.\n\nThe issues in the collection first appeared in 1991, 1992 and 1993. The collection first appeared in paperback and hardback in 1993.\n\nThe book contains four tales under the banner of &quot;Distant Mirrors&quot;, containing Issue #29 &quot;Thermidor&quot;, #30 &quot;August&quot;, #31 &quot;Three Septembers and a January&quot; and #50 &quot;Ramadan&quot;. Three of the issues making up the &quot;Distant Mirrors&quot; group were published between the &quot;Season of Mists&quot; and &quot;A Game of You&quot; story arcs. The last, &quot;Ramadan&quot;, was written contemporaneously, but because of art delays DC published it as Issue #50, after the &quot;Brief Lives&quot; arc.\n\nThree other issues appearing in Fables &amp; Reflections, published as the Convergence story &quot;arc&quot;, are also single-issue short stories. &quot;Convergence&quot; appeared between the &quot;A Game of You&quot; and &quot;Brief Lives&quot; story arcs. It contains Issues #38 &quot;The Hunt&quot;, #39 &quot;Soft Places&quot; and #40 &quot;The Parliament of Rooks&quot;.\n\nThe collection also includes the Sandman Special &quot;The Song of
